Fear-induced switch in synaptic AMPAR phenotype\n- A **single fear-inducing stimulus** triggers a transcription-dependent switch in synaptic **AMPAR phenotype** in cerebellar stellate cells, changing from GluR2-lacking (Ca$^{2+}$-permeable) to GluR2-containing (Ca$^{2+}$-impermeable) receptors within hours.\n- This switch is driven by **noradrenaline**, which prolongs action potentials, increases receptors** in the brain, especially in cerebellar stellate cells.\n- Activation of Œ≤-adrenergic receptors increases action potential duration and intracellular $Ca^{2+}$, triggering ERK/MAPK signaling and transcription of GluR2, leading to a switch in synaptic **AMPAR phenotype**.\n- This process enables a long-lasting change in synaptic strength and may underlie how emotional experiences modify learning and memory.\n\n### üìå Mechanism: Action potential broadening, Ca2+ entry, ERK/MAPK signaling, and GluR2 transcription\n- **Action potential broadening** increases **Ca$^{2+}$ entry** through L-type channels, activating the **ERK/MAPK signaling** pathway.\n- This cascade triggers **GluR2 gene transcription**, leading to more **GluR2-containing AMPARs** at synapses and a switch in receptor phenotype.\n\n### üìå Transcription-dependent vs. independent synaptic plasticity\n- **Transcription-dependent synaptic plasticity** involves long-lasting changes in synaptic strength that require new gene transcription, such as the noradrenaline-triggered switch to GluR2-containing AMPARs after fear stimuli.\n- **Transcription-independent synaptic plasticity** relies on rapid mechanisms like receptor trafficking or local protein synthesis, enabling fast changes in synaptic function without altering gene expression.\n- **Key difference:** Transcription-dependent changes occur over hours and affect the whole neuron, while transcription-independent changes happen within minutes and are often synapse-specific.\n\n### üìå Functional consequences for inhibitory neural circuits\n- **Inhibitory neural circuits** can undergo long-lasting changes in synaptic strength when a single fear-inducing stimulus triggers transcription-dependent upregulation of GluR2, leading to a switch in AMPAR phenotype.\n- This switch reduces **Ca$^{2+}$ permeability** at synapses, altering **synaptic efficacy** and potentially reshaping the activity of the entire inhibitory network.\n- **Property/Aspect** | **Description/Definition** | **Functional Significance** | \n|---------------------------|----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------|--------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------| \n| **Molecular Identity** | GluR2 is a subunit of AMPA-type glutamate receptors (AMPARs). [<9>][<1>] | It is essential for forming functional AMPARs that determine the specific properties of these receptors at synapses. [<9>][<4>] |\n| **Role in AMPAR Function**| AMPARs containing GluR2 are Ca$^{2+}$-impermeable, whereas receptors lacking GluR2 allow Ca$^{2+}$ influx. [<1>][<9>] | This role is critical because the presence of GluR2 limits Ca$^{2+}$ entry, thereby stabilizing synaptic transmission and protecting cells. [<1>][<4>] |\n| **Ca$^{2+}$ Permeability**| The incorporation of GluR2 into AMPARs prevents excessive Ca$^{2+}$ influx. [<1>][<6>] | By reducing Ca$^{2+}$ permeability, GluR2 helps maintain synaptic stability and protects against calcium-induced excitotoxicity. [<1>][<6>] |\n| **Transcriptional Regulation**| GluR2 expression is regulated at the transcriptional level, with factors such as emotional stress (e.g., fear) inducing an increase in GluR2 mRNA levels. [<2>][<8>] | This regulation promotes a switch from GluR2-lacking to GluR2-containing AMPARs, enabling activity-dependent synaptic modifications. [<2>][<8>] |\n| **Impact on Synaptic Plasticity**| The transcription-dependent switch in AMPAR subunit composition (from Ca$^{2+}$-permeable to Ca$^{2+}$-impermeable receptors) alters synaptic efficacy. [<2>][<9>] | This change underlies long-lasting adaptations in synaptic strength, which are fundamental for learning, memory, and the neuronal response to emotional stimuli. We found that a single fear-inducing stimulus increased GluR2 (also known as Gria2) mRNA abundance and promoted synaptic incorporation of GluR2-containing AMPA receptors (AMPARs) in mouse cerebellar stellate cells. entry, and activates the <strong>ERK/MAPK pathway</strong> to boost <em>GluR2</em> gene transcription.</li> <li>The result is a long-lasting change in synaptic function, potentially altering the activity of inhibitory neural networks in response to fear.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Role of noradrenaline and Œ≤-adrenergic receptors</h3> <ul> <li><strong>Noradrenaline</strong> released during emotional arousal activates <strong>Œ≤-adrenergic receptors</strong> in the brain, especially in cerebellar stellate cells.</li> <li>Activation of Œ≤-adrenergic receptors increases action potential duration and intracellular $Ca^{2+}$, triggering ERK/MAPK signaling and transcription of GluR2, leading to a switch in synaptic <strong>AMPAR phenotype</strong>.</li> <li>This process enables a long-lasting change in synaptic strength and may underlie how emotional experiences modify learning and memory.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Mechanism: Action potential broadening, Ca2+ entry, ERK/MAPK signaling, and GluR2 transcription</h3> <ul> <li><strong>Action potential broadening</strong> increases <strong>Ca&lt;sup&gt;2+&lt;/sup&gt; entry</strong> through L-type channels, activating the <strong>ERK/MAPK signaling</strong> pathway.</li> <li>This cascade triggers <strong>GluR2 gene transcription</strong>, leading to more <strong>GluR2-containing AMPARs</strong> at synapses and a switch in receptor phenotype.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Transcription-dependent vs. independent synaptic plasticity</h3> <ul> <li><strong>Transcription-dependent synaptic plasticity</strong> involves long-lasting changes in synaptic strength that require new gene transcription, such as the noradrenaline-triggered switch to GluR2-containing AMPARs after fear stimuli.</li> <li><strong>Transcription-independent synaptic plasticity</strong> relies on rapid mechanisms like receptor trafficking or local protein synthesis, enabling fast changes in synaptic function without altering gene expression.</li> <li><strong>Key difference:</strong> Transcription-dependent changes occur over hours and affect the whole neuron, while transcription-independent changes happen within minutes and are often synapse-specific.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Functional consequences for inhibitory neural circuits</h3> <ul> <li><strong>Inhibitory neural circuits</strong> can undergo long-lasting changes in synaptic strength when a single fear-inducing stimulus triggers transcription-dependent upregulation of GluR2, leading to a switch in AMPAR phenotype.</li> <li>This switch reduces <strong>Ca&lt;sup&gt;2+&lt;/sup&gt; permeability</strong> at synapses, altering <strong>synaptic efficacy</strong> and potentially reshaping the activity of the entire inhibitory network.</li> <li><strong>Noradrenaline</strong> and <strong>action potential broadening</strong> are key signals that activate the <strong>ERK/MAPK pathway</strong>, driving these transcriptional and functional changes.</li> </ul> <hr> <h3>üí¨ Ask Me Anything!</h3> <p>Feel free to ask me any questions about the document! influx, and ERK/MAPK signaling‚Äîthat increases synaptic incorporation of GluR2-containing, Ca&lt;sup&gt;2+&lt;/sup&gt;-impermeable <strong>AMPA receptors</strong>.</strong></p> <h3>üìö Document Overview</h3> <ul> <li><strong>A single fear-inducing stimulus</strong> triggers a long-lasting, transcription-dependent switch in synaptic <strong>AMPA receptor (AMPAR) phenotype</strong> in cerebellar stellate cells.</li> <li>This switch involves increased <strong>GluR2 mRNA</strong> and synaptic incorporation of <strong>GluR2-containing, Ca&lt;sup&gt;2+&lt;/sup&gt;-impermeable AMPARs</strong>, replacing GluR2-lacking, Ca&lt;sup&gt;2+&lt;/sup&gt;-permeable receptors.</li> <li>The process is mediated by <strong>noradrenaline</strong>, which prolongs action potentials, enhances Ca&lt;sup&gt;2+&lt;/sup&gt; entry, and activates <strong>ERK/MAPK signaling</strong> to drive new GluR2 gene transcription.</li> <li>This <strong>phenotype switch</strong> alters synaptic efficacy and may reshape inhibitory neural network activity in response to emotional stress.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Fear-induced switch in synaptic AMPAR phenotype</h3> <ul> <li>A <strong>single fear-inducing stimulus</strong> triggers a transcription-dependent switch in synaptic <strong>AMPAR phenotype</strong> in cerebellar stellate cells, changing from GluR2-lacking (Ca&lt;sup&gt;2+&lt;/sup&gt;-permeable) to GluR2-containing (Ca&lt;sup&gt;2+&lt;/sup&gt;-impermeable) receptors within hours.</li> <li>This switch is driven by <strong>noradrenaline</strong>, which prolongs action potentials, increases Ca&lt;sup&gt;2+&lt;/sup&gt; entry, and activates the <strong>ERK/MAPK pathway</strong> to boost <em>GluR2</em> gene transcription.</li> <li>The result is a long-lasting change in synaptic function, potentially altering the activity of inhibitory neural networks in response to fear.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Role of noradrenaline and Œ≤-adrenergic receptors</h3> <ul> <li><strong>Noradrenaline</strong> released during emotional arousal activates <strong>Œ≤-adrenergic receptors</strong> in the brain, especially in cerebellar stellate cells.</li> <li>Activation of Œ≤-adrenergic receptors increases action potential duration and intracellular $Ca^{2+}$, triggering ERK/MAPK signaling and transcription of GluR2, leading to a switch in synaptic <strong>AMPAR phenotype</strong>.</li> <li>This process enables a long-lasting change in synaptic strength and may underlie how emotional experiences modify learning and memory.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Mechanism: Action potential broadening, Ca2+ entry, ERK/MAPK signaling, and GluR2 transcription</h3> <ul> <li><strong>Action potential broadening</strong> increases <strong>Ca&lt;sup&gt;2+&lt;/sup&gt; entry</strong> through L-type channels, activating the <strong>ERK/MAPK signaling</strong> pathway.</li> <li>This cascade triggers <strong>GluR2 gene transcription</strong>, leading to more <strong>GluR2-containing AMPARs</strong> at synapses and a switch in receptor phenotype.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Transcription-dependent vs. independent synaptic plasticity</h3> <ul> <li><strong>Transcription-dependent synaptic plasticity</strong> involves long-lasting changes in synaptic strength that require new gene transcription, such as the noradrenaline-triggered switch to GluR2-containing AMPARs after fear stimuli.</li> <li><strong>Transcription-independent synaptic plasticity</strong> relies on rapid mechanisms like receptor trafficking or local protein synthesis, enabling fast changes in synaptic function without altering gene expression.</li> <li><strong>Key difference:</strong> Transcription-dependent changes occur over hours and affect the whole neuron, while transcription-independent changes happen within minutes and are often synapse-specific.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Functional consequences for inhibitory neural circuits</h3> <ul> <li><strong>Inhibitory neural circuits</strong> can undergo long-lasting changes in synaptic strength when a single fear-inducing stimulus triggers transcription-dependent upregulation of GluR2, leading to a switch in AMPAR phenotype.</li> <li>This switch reduces <strong>Ca&lt;sup&gt;2+&lt;/sup&gt; permeability</strong> at synapses, altering <strong>synaptic efficacy</strong> and potentially reshaping the activity of the entire inhibitory network.</li> <li><strong>Noradrenaline</strong> and <strong>action potential broadening</strong> are key signals that activate the <strong>ERK/MAPK pathway</strong>, driving these transcriptional and functional changes.</li> </ul> <hr/> <h3>üí¨ Ask Me Anything!</h3> <p>Feel free to ask me any questions about the document!
